  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bec8213a-b6e1-4972-9850-a59024031ce8'

<span id="freeTextContainer11602849532166050056">Originally published in 1925, this book became known for the frank sexuality of its contents and its account of middle class French morality. The themes of the book explore the problem of morals, the problem of society and the problems facing writers. An appendix to this edition (Vintage, 1973) contains excerpts from the Gide's notebooks which he kept while writing this bo</span>
  <span id="freeText11602849532166050056" style="display:none">Originally published in 1925, this book became known for the frank sexuality of its contents and its account of middle class French morality. The themes of the book explore the problem of morals, the problem of society and the problems facing writers. An appendix to this edition (Vintage, 1973) contains excerpts from the Gide's notebooks which he kept while writing this book.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132816983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11856593638007625975">Les faux-Monnayeurs = The Counterfeiters, André Gide (André Paul Guillaume Gide)<br/>The Counterfeiters, is a 1925 novel by French author André Gide. The Counterfeiters, adorn three parts. Part One: The events in this section take place in Paris, in the author's childhood alleys. Part II: It happens in one of the Swiss villages. Third part: It happens again in Paris; the story time is not more than five months; from the end of spring to the end of the story; and the story takes place before the First</span>
  <span id="freeText11856593638007625975" style="display:none">Les faux-Monnayeurs = The Counterfeiters, André Gide (André Paul Guillaume Gide)<br />The Counterfeiters, is a 1925 novel by French author André Gide. The Counterfeiters, adorn three parts. Part One: The events in this section take place in Paris, in the author's childhood alleys. Part II: It happens in one of the Swiss villages. Third part: It happens again in Paris; the story time is not more than five months; from the end of spring to the end of the story; and the story takes place before the First World War. <br />The plot revolves around Bernard, a school friend of Olivier's. With many characters and crisscrossing plot lines. The Counterfeiters is a novel-within-a-novel, with Edouard (the alter ego of Gide) intending to write a book of the same title. Other stylistic devices are also used, such as an omniscient narrator who sometimes addresses the reader directly, weighs in on the characters' motivations or discusses alternate realities. Therefore, the book has been seen as a precursor of the new roman. The structure of the novel was written to mirror "Cubism," in that it interweaves between several different plots and portrays multiple points of view. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه دسامبر سال 1973 میلادی<br />عنوان: سکه سازان؛ نویسنده: آندره ژید (آندره پل گیوم ژید؛ حسن فرهمندی؛ تهران، امیرکبیر، 1335؛ در 400 ص، عکس، چاپ سوم 1349؛ ویرایشهای دیگر با مقدمه و حواشی در 667 ص؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، کتابفروشی زوار، 1359، در 546 ص؛ چاپ پنجم؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، ماهی، 1386، در 479 ص؛ چاپ دوم ماهی 1388؛ چاپ سوم 1392؛ شابک: 9789649971223؛<br /><br />سکه سازان را سه بخش آراسته؛ بخش نخست: در 232 ص است، رخدادهای این بخش، در پاریس، و در کوچه های کودکی نویسنده، میگذرد؛ بخش دوم: در 76 ص است، در یکی از دهکده های سوئیس، روی میدهد. بخش سوم: در 236 ص، باز هم در پاریس روی میدهد؛ زمان داستان بیش از پنج ماه نیست؛ از پایان بهار، تا پایان پائیز؛ و تاریخ رخداد داستان، پیش از جنگ جهانگیر نخست است؛ سکه سازان یک رمان انتقادی ست؛ اشخاص داستان: سی و پنج تن هستند، از هرگونه آدمی در بین آنها هست؛ برنار مضطرب؛ آرمان منحرف؛ ونسان مردد؛ کشیشان، معلمان، پدران و مادران فرمانبر شیوه های کهن؛ و یک زن استثنایی، شیک پوش، زیبا، ثروتمند و لیدی گریفیث (لیلیان)؛ ایشان به خدا و شیطان، به هر دو، اعتنا ندارد؛ و موجب نومیدی نویسنده نیز، هست. ادوار، هم بازیگر ست، هم تماشاگر؛ و هم داستان نویس؛ ... در طول رمان، دو داستان ادامه مییابد، یکی واقعی و رئال، و دیگری ایده آل و سمبولیک. بدینگونه ژید دوگانگی انسان را میبیند و ....؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_51515141" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51515141" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51515141?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/40168385-the-counterfeiters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=gay-fiction">gay-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=read-and-reread">read-and-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51515141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9963936108632987957">The Counterfeiters is a book about writing a book, also called "The Counterfeiters". That is the primary theme of the novel which comes from the title of the book by the writer Edouard. Thus The Counterfeiters is a novel-within-a-novel, with Edouard (the alter ego of Gide) writing a book of the same title. Other stylistic devices are also used, such as an omniscient narrator that sometimes addresses the reader directly, weighs in on the characters' motivations or discusses alternate realities. H</span>
  <span id="freeText9963936108632987957" style="display:none">The Counterfeiters is a book about writing a book, also called "The Counterfeiters". That is the primary theme of the novel which comes from the title of the book by the writer Edouard. Thus The Counterfeiters is a novel-within-a-novel, with Edouard (the alter ego of Gide) writing a book of the same title. Other stylistic devices are also used, such as an omniscient narrator that sometimes addresses the reader directly, weighs in on the characters' motivations or discusses alternate realities. However, there is also the story of a group of boys who are passing counterfeit coins throughout Paris. Thus we have entered a world where we cannot trust our senses -- what is counterfeit and what is real? <br /><br />The story of Edouard writing his novel demonstrates his search for knowledge, yet as he associates with a group of his own adolescent relatives it appears as an artificial arrangement; one that displays the effects upon society of youth's corruption of traditional standards and values. The collapse of morality is illustrated with Eduoard's nephew Vincent, who deserts his lover Laura, a married woman, and runs away with Lillian, the mistress of Count Robert de Passavant. His life goes downhill as he murders her and goes insane. <br />There is also the coming of age story of Bernard and Olivier as they prepare to leave school -- but does this extend beyond their education and emanate from all who are learning about the world? This learning which is required by the changing nature of the everyday, the quotidian reality that is, perhaps, counterfeit.<br /><br />I found the details of Edouard's struggles with his career, his family, his friendships and love provide images that enhance the main themes, yet also provide narrative drive. Another subplot of the novel is homosexuality. Some of the characters are overtly homosexual, like the adolescent Olivier, and the adult writers Count de Passavant and Eduoard. The Count seems to be an evil and corrupting force while the latter is benevolent. Even when the treatment is not overt, there is a homoerotic subtext that runs throughout, which encompasses Olivier's friend, Bernard, and their schoolfellows Gontran and Philippe. The main theme of The Counterfeiters underlies the issue of sexuality, morality, and social order and lineage in a unique way for his era. <br /><br />Gide's novel was not received well on its appearance, perhaps because of its homosexual themes and its unusual composition. It is this unusual composition that I thought made it an interesting read; along with which the way Gide demonstrates ideas through his characters and their actions much like Dostoevsky and Thomas Mann. The Counterfeiters has improved its reputation in the intervening years and is now generally counted among the great novels of the twentieth century.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9963936108632987957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_720957953" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/720957953" />
    <a title="Bethan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040996-bethan"><img alt="Bethan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475468854p2/1040996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/720957953?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bethan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bethan" href="/user/show/1040996-bethan">Bethan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140180930" href="/book/show/70106.The_Counterfeiters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040996-bethan?shelf=lgbt-interest">lgbt-interest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040996-bethan?shelf=regular-fiction">regular-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer720957953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14285611206392666161">This is subtle metafiction since one of the main characters is a writer and the nature of Gide's <i>The Counterfeiters</i> is intellectual, bohemian, philosophical and of its time - for example, Freudian techniques used on a boy are exposited upon and discussed. It follows various people of a social milieu, from schoolboys to new school-leavers, to their parents and relatives, to an old schoolmaster, and is about their relationships and connections: in that sense, it was soap-like. It is also very homo</span>
  <span id="freeText14285611206392666161" style="display:none">This is subtle metafiction since one of the main characters is a writer and the nature of Gide's <i>The Counterfeiters</i> is intellectual, bohemian, philosophical and of its time - for example, Freudian techniques used on a boy are exposited upon and discussed. It follows various people of a social milieu, from schoolboys to new school-leavers, to their parents and relatives, to an old schoolmaster, and is about their relationships and connections: in that sense, it was soap-like. It is also very homoerotic - never directly stated but some of the male main characters are almost certainly gay. <br /><br />It wasn't the easiest of books to follow but it was good in an interesting way. The denouement (cannot spoil), however, I did not like much because it felt a bit obvious and heavy-handed to me and it rang hollow, so that was a shame.<br /><br />Edit: I suppose the counterfeit coins used in the novel that the title references, represent the hollow and somewhat flashy or surface nature and currency of many of these relationships people have with each other. I will say that I did find the novel a bit depressing but almost scientifically honest that way because it kind of mirrors what I see and experience about people. I'm sure that says more about me though. Again, I think this is an interesting and philosophical novel - it wove subtly complex webs across my mind. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14285611206392666161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_469246209" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/469246209" />
    <a title="Gregg Bell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15148376-gregg-bell"><img alt="Gregg Bell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558922294p2/15148376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/469246209?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregg Bell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregg Bell" href="/user/show/15148376-gregg-bell">Gregg Bell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer469246209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14453778622045623540"><i>The Counterfeiters</i> is a book for writers, intellectuals or learners. It's an odd book with crisscrossing themes and story lines. Difficult to follow. (I gave up trying to follow it.) Gide, a winner of the Nobel prize for literature, is an old-time writer, the emphasis being on "writer," rather than "novelist." In those days, there weren't that many writers around and writers often were able to put whatever they wanted in their books.<br/><br/>And Gide put a lot in this book. It's just not much of a story.</span>
  <span id="freeText14453778622045623540" style="display:none"><i>The Counterfeiters</i> is a book for writers, intellectuals or learners. It's an odd book with crisscrossing themes and story lines. Difficult to follow. (I gave up trying to follow it.) Gide, a winner of the Nobel prize for literature, is an old-time writer, the emphasis being on "writer," rather than "novelist." In those days, there weren't that many writers around and writers often were able to put whatever they wanted in their books.<br /><br />And Gide put a lot in this book. It's just not much of a story. Ostensibly it's about a young artist's search for artistic authenticity. But that search gets twisted in a hundred different directions, ending up only as strands heading in a general direction. And yet in the process much insightful information about art and the artistic process is provided. And information about life. <br /><br />Consider these gems:<br /><br />"It very often suffices to add together a quantity of little facts which, taken separately, are very simple and very natural, to arrive at a sum which is monstrous."<br /><br />"...the greatest intelligence is precisely the one that suffers most from its own limitations."<br /><br />Like a Renaissance man, Gide's thoughts are learned and broad ranging, and yet clearly the book's predominant emphasis is on art, more specifically writing. Even, in this edition anyway, at the end, there is Gide's notebook on his process of how he wrote the novel. And it's good information. Like, "...be guided by the words rather than force them into submission."<br /><br />So, read this novel for entertainment? I would say not really. Read it to read a brilliant man and learn from him? You're getting warmer. Read it to learn about art and writing? You're hot now.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Have a little spare time? I've free flash fiction (all stories under 1K) at my website greggbell.net </span>
  <a data-text-id="14453778622045623540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_947729501" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/947729501" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/947729501?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/817280.Les_Faux_monnayeurs">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Protestants looking for a Christian Catcher in the Rye</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer947729501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10474484324050263524">The reputation of the Faux-Monnayeurs is that it is an experimental novel that succeeds only where it is conventional. Gide's experimentation comes in the form of a magically real angel, multiple narrative points of view combined with more subplots and characters than would normally be considered prudent in a well-controlled work.<br/><br/>At 19, I liked it because the author maintained a rapid pace and clearly had great affection for his characters. As well the discussion on homosexuality was new to me. </span>
  <span id="freeText10474484324050263524" style="display:none">The reputation of the Faux-Monnayeurs is that it is an experimental novel that succeeds only where it is conventional. Gide's experimentation comes in the form of a magically real angel, multiple narrative points of view combined with more subplots and characters than would normally be considered prudent in a well-controlled work.<br /><br />At 19, I liked it because the author maintained a rapid pace and clearly had great affection for his characters. As well the discussion on homosexuality was new to me. Because Gide obviously knew the topic very well I felt that the Faux-Monnayeurs delivered great insight.<br /><br />I was stunned when an angel appeared in the novel to wrestle with Bernard as if he were Jacob. I was then very glad to see that Bernard was subsequently able to make peace with his adopted father.<br /><br />The novel however ends on a very cruel note as a bullied child commits suicide in front of his classmates. I had frankly hoped that the Angel could have saved the little boy and still regret that he did not.<br /><br />This is a great novel about the soul-searching of adolescent males during their late teens. I recommend it well before Catcher in the Rye. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10474484324050263524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_115994257" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115994257" />
    <a title="Chris Blocker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/318724-chris-blocker"><img alt="Chris Blocker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460411481p2/318724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115994257?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Blocker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Blocker" href="/user/show/318724-chris-blocker">Chris Blocker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/318724-chris-blocker?shelf=to-read-2013">to-read-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115994257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14792054354574820856"><i>Steinbeck made me do it.</i><br/><br/>Years ago, while working on my thesis for my master's degree, I learned everything there was to know about <i>East of Eden</i>. One bit of information I learned was the books that had influenced Steinbeck, particularly in his writing of <i>EoE</i>. There were titles I'd heard of: <i>Moby-Dick</i> and <i>Don Quixote</i>. And there was this: <i>The Counterfeiters</i>. Because I loved all things <i>East of Eden</i>, I made it a point to read these influential books.<br/><br/>Now that I've read <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, I see how it</span>
  <span id="freeText14792054354574820856" style="display:none"><i>Steinbeck made me do it.</i><br><br>Years ago, while working on my thesis for my master's degree, I learned everything there was to know about <i>East of Eden</i>. One bit of information I learned was the books that had influenced Steinbeck, particularly in his writing of <i>EoE</i>. There were titles I'd heard of: <i>Moby-Dick</i> and <i>Don Quixote</i>. And there was this: <i>The Counterfeiters</i>. Because I loved all things <i>East of Eden</i>, I made it a point to read these influential books.<br><br>Now that I've read <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, I see how it influenced Steinbeck. <i>The Counterfeiters</i> is an intelligent and imaginative novel. And it is perhaps the earliest example of post-modernist metafiction I've encountered. Here is a novel called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, a novel which includes a character who is an author writing a book named <i>The Counterfeiters</i>. And as the character in the novel shapes the story, so changes the story in the reader's hands. Lovely. I thoroughly enjoy works like this. (Steinbeck's original draft of <i>East of Eden</i> featured some similar metafictional elements, but many of these were toned down at the insistence of Steinbeck's editor and publisher.)<br><br>Also groundbreaking was Gide's approach to sexuality. The majority of the characters in the novel are either gay or bisexual. Their relationships are presented realistically—in both positive and negative light—just like you'd expect from any heterosexual relationship. Published in 1925, this display of authentic, non-sensationalized homosexual relationships was surely an original take.<br><br>So <i>The Counterfeiters</i> was definitely a groundbreaking novel, but oh, is it void of anything enjoyable. It's intelligent and creative, but there's not much of a story. It is art for the sake of art. And it's so incredibly hard to follow. <i>The Counterfeiters</i> has a cast of characters that rivals <i>War and Peace</i>; the difference is that <i>The Counterfeiters</i> introduces these character in a third of the space Tolstoy allowed for his characters to develop. Another big difference, <i>War and Peace</i> was vastly interesting.<br><br>While trying to follow this novel, I stumbled upon the following graphic. I think it nicely illustrates one of the problems I had with <i>The Counterfeiters</i>.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548690744i/26981312._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>graphic by <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/User:Morn" rel="nofollow noopener">Morn the Gorn</a></i><br>What you see here is the cast of characters, and their convoluted relationships. It's not unlike a graphic you might find for <i>War and Peace</i>. Halfway through this novel, though, I couldn't honestly tell you more than one or two details about no more than two or three of these characters. Many of the others were recognizable by name only. Looking at this diagram should have made the story easier to read, but it didn't, and that's purely because Gide made a much too tangled mess of a novel. Honestly, and I hate admitting this, it got so bad that I began to skim pages and skip entire passages. I just didn't enjoy anything about this story.<br><br>To Gide's credit, however, this novel was definitely groundbreaking. For me, the way it shattered the confines of story and sexuality were the only redeeming qualities. I'm glad he wrote it, if for no other reason than it changed the landscape of the modern novel, but I have no desire to read this novel ever again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14792054354574820856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12838489118854918649" style="display:none">Ah, God bless the French! <div id="review_31910418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31910418" />
    <a title="Dusty Myers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/423999-dusty-myers"><img alt="Dusty Myers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190841956p2/423999.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31910418?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dusty Myers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dusty Myers" href="/user/show/423999-dusty-myers">Dusty Myers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31910418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7744605255597021232">A novel as much about writing as it is about coded homosexuality in 1920's France (a time, lest we forget, that Gertrude Stein and Alice B. Toklas just about ruled that town). Two rival writers, Edouard and Robert, fall for the same impressionable young boy, Olivier, who decides to run off with Robert, the more famous and less honorable of the two. Edouard, a kind of stand-in for Gide, is Olivier's "uncle" (through marriage), and in the loss of his beloved nephew opts instead of his schoolfriend</span>
  <span id="freeText7744605255597021232" style="display:none">A novel as much about writing as it is about coded homosexuality in 1920's France (a time, lest we forget, that Gertrude Stein and Alice B. Toklas just about ruled that town). Two rival writers, Edouard and Robert, fall for the same impressionable young boy, Olivier, who decides to run off with Robert, the more famous and less honorable of the two. Edouard, a kind of stand-in for Gide, is Olivier's "uncle" (through marriage), and in the loss of his beloved nephew opts instead of his schoolfriend, Bernard. The intersections that follow among writing instruction, publishing, entry into adulthood, and sexuality are noteworthy, but not too.<br /><br />Also noteworthy is the form of this novel. It reads precisely like something out of Austen. Many of the novels are structured around the meeting of two characters. Even the titles indicate such: "Bernard and Olivier", "Vincent Meets Passavant at Lady Griffith's", Bernard Meets Olivier", etc. The novel has that 19th-century breadth of including everyone from the aristocracy to the poor girl pregnant out of wedlock. And this in 1925! <i>The Great Gatsby</i> came out in 1925!<br /><br />I can't quite figure it out. There's a strong anti-decadent bent in this novel. Robert, the "comte" who drags Olivier to the dark side, is painted as a kind of Wildean figure, which is interesting given the friendship shared between Wilde and Gide. It's only when Olivier realizes he needs to abandon Robert and side himself with a more modernistic writer like his uncle that he is safe. Perhaps Gide's trying to resurrect an approach to the novel the decadents tried to do away with. I mean the novel ends with a boy's accidental suicide, out of the blue. It's practically right out of Dickens, but like act two of Dickens....</span>
  <a data-text-id="7744605255597021232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_64634949" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64634949" />
    <a title="Kit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2310268-kit"><img alt="Kit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251048937p2/2310268.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64634949?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kit" href="/user/show/2310268-kit">Kit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2310268-kit?shelf=library-books">library-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2310268-kit?shelf=french-or-some-effort">french-or-some-effort</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64634949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12162924799948858523">This book I couldn't figure out. The feeling I get after reading it was similar to the time I read American Psycho. On the surface, you wonder, what's going on? when is this going to get interesting? And you might leave disappointed. There's something subliminal about the Counterfeiters that is beyond my intelligence to figure out. The characters are meshed together in one book, which seem to act as a pressure cooker. Gide, instead of letting the plot twists and turns let the story flow as the c</span>
  <span id="freeText12162924799948858523" style="display:none">This book I couldn't figure out. The feeling I get after reading it was similar to the time I read American Psycho. On the surface, you wonder, what's going on? when is this going to get interesting? And you might leave disappointed. There's something subliminal about the Counterfeiters that is beyond my intelligence to figure out. The characters are meshed together in one book, which seem to act as a pressure cooker. Gide, instead of letting the plot twists and turns let the story flow as the characters lead them to, following their respective personalities and conflicts. Edouard's journal's, his efforts with HIS own version of the Counterfeiters, how much of that reflects the novel? Which begs the question: how mcuh of it reflects Gide's life, at least in the time of writing?<br /><br />The form of the novel is unique, depending on which version you pick up. It's probably better to pick up the one with Gide's own journals, and also the appendix. (One of) the story is about writing about writing. So it follows, with Gide's journals, the book is about writing about writing about writing, among its many themes. Gide tried to fit as much as he knew in one book. That in itself should be respected. It does tend to get messy at times due to Gide's ambitions, but you should just press on.<br /><br />The English version translation I got out from my library (the only one) by Bussy wasn't great. But I'm not sure what other translations are like. If you could read French, though, that may be the best asset for reading this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12162924799948858523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1747984074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1747984074" />
    <a title="Charlie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22451092-charlie"><img alt="Charlie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595429567p2/22451092.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1747984074?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlie" href="/user/show/22451092-charlie">Charlie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070349609" href="/book/show/13556545-les-faux-monnayeurs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1747984074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1413993162142743975">I finally finished it and am now going to read the journal the author kept while writing this. I mean it's a very long story about writing stories with a lot of characters and sometimes it feels that the line between what the character's are thinking and the what the author is thinking and what the author is thinking of the actual real novel is thin. Supposedly he let the story flow naturally and yet I have a hard time imagining that he could have done so because there are so many little things </span>
  <span id="freeText1413993162142743975" style="display:none">I finally finished it and am now going to read the journal the author kept while writing this. I mean it's a very long story about writing stories with a lot of characters and sometimes it feels that the line between what the character's are thinking and the what the author is thinking and what the author is thinking of the actual real novel is thin. Supposedly he let the story flow naturally and yet I have a hard time imagining that he could have done so because there are so many little things that needed to happen to get to the end result. Maybe I can't fathom it because when I write I need to pre-plan most every important thing...<br /><br />It's a heavy read and still the writing style drags your eyes along the pages with force. You want to go forward and get closer to the characters perhaps because they're so beautifully painted. <br /><br />Maybe I will edit this later and make a clearer review when I will have studied the book in depth but I don't think there is a much deeper meaning. I am not sure that this is a book with a message per say. <br /><br />It's got a soft feel to it. Overall I do recommend it a lot I think although, and the characters employ this very expression in the book to talk about one of the writer's book (do you follow me there because I think I lose myself in the bookception?) it's just a "slice of life".</span>
  <a data-text-id="1413993162142743975" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_679556226" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/679556226" />
    <a title="Khadijah Qamar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13922142-khadijah-qamar"><img alt="Khadijah Qamar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374347449p2/13922142.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/679556226?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khadijah Qamar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khadijah Qamar" href="/user/show/13922142-khadijah-qamar">Khadijah Qamar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13922142-khadijah-qamar?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer679556226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15660275260308896811">Gide's novel has moments of keen psychological penetration and, for that reason alone, deserves veritable accolades. It's especially informative and interesting to be witness to the progress of the novel in the Appendix, which reads as Gide's diary throughout the writing of The Counterfeiters. Like other reviewers have noted, it breaks from the "traditional" novel mold since it is a novel about writing a novel. Gide also challenges novelistic norms at the time by changing perspectives, which he </span>
  <span id="freeText15660275260308896811" style="display:none">Gide's novel has moments of keen psychological penetration and, for that reason alone, deserves veritable accolades. It's especially informative and interesting to be witness to the progress of the novel in the Appendix, which reads as Gide's diary throughout the writing of The Counterfeiters. Like other reviewers have noted, it breaks from the "traditional" novel mold since it is a novel about writing a novel. Gide also challenges novelistic norms at the time by changing perspectives, which he describes in his appendix as an innovative attempt to directly involve the reader in piecing together real events from multiple subjective first-person narratives. I'm not sure that it always succeeds well, since this style contributes to the lack of any significant plot and an unsatisfying conclusion. The supposed climax of the novel ends in a tragic denouement involving a seemingly secondary character. Meanwhile, the threads of the primary characters, who I assume to be Olivier, Bernard, and Edouard, neatly sort themselves out without any significant developments, excluding some juvenile angst on the part of Olivier. <br /><br />This novel may have been controversial and significant in its time, particularly in regards to its unique stylistic exposition and the obvious homosexuality of most of the characters, but I do not think it is a novel "for all ages" as Gide might have wished. In conclusion, it's more of a "slice of life" narrative rather than a novel, and although Gide has moments of clever insights, in the end the story will likely leave the reader under-served. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15660275260308896811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_180072848" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180072848" />
    <a title="Tyler " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1096417-tyler"><img alt="Tyler " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377822301p2/1096417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180072848?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler " href="/user/show/1096417-tyler">Tyler </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Literature</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096417-tyler?shelf=early-gay-lit">early-gay-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180072848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7541489853792998713">Billed as a frank exploration of same-sex deviance and an exposition of the collapse of morals in 1920’s France, this book is not quite either. It’s a transitional work. Readers wanting a frank look at gay sex are referred to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106096.Death_on_the_Installment_Plan' title='Death on the Installment Plan by Louis-Ferdinand Céline' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death on the Installment Plan</a>, where Celine’s two or three shocking vignettes on the subject have never been outdone. And since kids getting away with anything is the grist of modern media, the general state of morality doesn’t especially shock modern readers. <br/><br/>But the trans</span>
  <span id="freeText7541489853792998713" style="display:none">Billed as a frank exploration of same-sex deviance and an exposition of the collapse of morals in 1920’s France, this book is not quite either. It’s a transitional work. Readers wanting a frank look at gay sex are referred to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106096.Death_on_the_Installment_Plan" title="Death on the Installment Plan by Louis-Ferdinand Céline" rel="nofollow noopener">Death on the Installment Plan</a>, where Celine’s two or three shocking vignettes on the subject have never been outdone. And since kids getting away with anything is the grist of modern media, the general state of morality doesn’t especially shock modern readers. <br /><br />But the transitional nature of the story is interesting. It reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126512.The_Way_of_All_Flesh" title="The Way of All Flesh by Samuel Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">The Way of All Flesh</a>, a 19th century bildungsroman centering on an uncle and his nephew, with winks to the discerning reader. Gide develops this idea in a French setting and adds more characters of uncertain virtue. Somewhere in my reading, in fact, I began assuming any male character was gay until proven otherwise. I later exonerated three of them and found four others guilty of sending coded messages without an encryption device. The author pushes the story beyond innuendo without going as far as Celine would a decade later.<br /><br />How much the book scandalized people at the time I don‘t know. It went on the market about when Josephine Baker was in Paris dancing naked except for a string of bananas around her waist, so I imagine the French public was ready as well for something provocative by way of literature. Yet no <i>explicit</i> sex takes place. What we have is in large part a sentimental narration of the characters. <br /><br />But that’s not it either, exactly. The story depicts a snooty literary artistic clique more so than a debaucherous cabal, and the characters are trying to out-snoot each other as much as anything. The narrative action is limited and the story moves along with the help of letters and notes interspersed within the prose. The book is a campy act much in the spirit of the movie <i>All About Eve</i>.<br /><br />In <i>The Counterfeiters</i> I saw that the author took care with everything from the structure of the narrative to the word choices in individual paragraphs. I appreciated the superior content, and I fell in easily with the book's transitional nature, imagining Gide as an artist caught between genres. I didn’t rate this tale higher because I found the focus on the literati too confining. But that’s a matter of personal taste. I recommend the book to people interested in the themes as well as to people in search of good literature.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="7541489853792998713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_921142620" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/921142620" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9592279-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533140387p2/9592279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/921142620?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/9592279-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer921142620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6935559345978905491"><i>"To disturb is my function. The public always prefers to be reassured. There are those whose job this is. There are only too many."</i><br/><br/>Those words sound like something Andy Warhol might have said (in fact he said something very much like it). Indeed, <i>The Counterfeiters</i> (<i>Les Faux-monnayeurs</i>) is deeply disturbing on many levels, especially when read in today's socio-political climate of Me-Too and Time's-Up, though pederasty was considered wrong long before this. Andre Gide, a public defender of peder</span>
  <span id="freeText6935559345978905491" style="display:none"><i>"To disturb is my function. The public always prefers to be reassured. There are those whose job this is. There are only too many."</i><br /><br />Those words sound like something Andy Warhol might have said (in fact he said something very much like it). Indeed, <i>The Counterfeiters</i> (<i>Les Faux-monnayeurs</i>) is deeply disturbing on many levels, especially when read in today's socio-political climate of Me-Too and Time's-Up, though pederasty was considered wrong long before this. Andre Gide, a public defender of pederasty, makes it abundantly clear in his notebooks that his protagonist in this work, Edouard, is only a very lightly-veiled version of himself. When one dives deep into the biography of Gide, one finds a very troubling past, including numerous sexual liaisons with young boys (sometimes in the company of Oscar Wilde). Gide won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1947, which to those of us today who believe in consent and argue against an abuse of power seems extraordinarily problematic. But, of course, the Nobel Peace Prize has been awarded to warmongers and awards are generally meaningless anyhow, a mixture of politics and favoritism, not always but often devoid of merit. There was something beyond repugnant in the idea of a middle-aged man falling for and sleeping with his adolescent nephew, especially knowing that art here was very much a representation of the author's real life. <br /><br />This might seem like enough cause to dismiss <i>The Counterfeiters</i> and similar works by Gide like <i>The Immoralist</i>, just as others have done in their readings of Nabokov (<i>Lolita</i>'s Humbert Humbert is in many ways the equivalent of Edouard in this work, just as is Charles Kinbote in <i>Pale Fire</i>; Nabokov's indebtedness to both Gide's themes and style has been noted in a few pieces of literary criticism). The shocking ending, with a bit of Melville's <i>Billy Budd</i>, might also turn many away from this work, if they make it so far. I admit that I nearly put the work down, and very likely would have if it were explicit in its treatment of the relations between Edouard and his nephew Olivier, and later his nephew and the corrupting Comte de Passavant. <br /><br />But (and here's where it's tricky), while the work disturbs and repulses many a reader (especially the modern reader), it has a marvelous stylistic appeal (just as do the works of Nabokov), that if not for its content and knowing that it was largely autobiographical would have warranted a five star review. Throw in a little of Balzac ("possibly our greatest novelist," writes Gide in his journals; there would have been considerably more Balzac had Gide written the book as originally designed, featuring prominently Lafcadio, a character from his earlier works), a touch of Cervantes, a bit of Saint-Simon, a little Stendhal, and a medley of philosophical and psychological ideas (nationalist, Catholic, socialist, Freudian, William James) and mix it vigorously and you might get something like <i>The Counterfeiters</i>. <br /><br />A <i>mise en abyme</i>, the story tells of Edouard the young artist, writing a book called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, keeping a journal that mixes elements of his life with ideas about his art. Not unlike films like <i>Citizen Kane</i> and <i>Rashomon</i> the story also presents us various, sometimes conflicting, viewpoints as it weaves together a sort of narrative of good and evil, the decay of middle class morals (Gide is a moralist as much as, if not more than anything else), a philosophy of art. We hear about things at times from a playful omniscient narrator (similar to Kundera's style in <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>, but not as precious; at the end of Part II, about halfway through the book, the narrator [Gide himself?] pauses to reflect on the characters, how they developed on their own, what he thinks may become of them, what regrets he has in the way they came about, etc.), at other times through letters and journal entries from the characters (reminiscent of Bram Stoker's <i>Dracula</i>). <br /><br />In his journals for <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, Gide, that champion of individualism (a la Whitman, Rimbaud, Nietzsche), makes known his dislike for the style of Tolstoy. And I couldn't help think as I read it that Tolstoy would have very likely found this work repugnant. To Tolstoy (as expounded in his <i>What is Art?</i>), good art draws on a Christian ethic and on feelings of universal fraternity. I don't fully agree with this, but it is obvious that these elements are lacking from Gide's novel. <br /><br />On the whole, I would not say that Tolstoy was a man who liked to reassure his readers, but he does this more than he disturbs, such was his philosophy. In works like <i>Resurrection</i> his critiques of society are damning, even if he concludes with a philosophy of how to make the world a better place. Both Gide and Tolstoy rejected the religious tradition in which they were brought up (the Russian Orthodox Church for Tolstoy, Calvinism for Gide). And both (I know this more with Tolstoy, having read a great many of his works, but have read it about Gide) continued to have religious dialogues in their works, as their characters grappled with matters of faith, God, morals and questions of good and evil. Tolstoy settled on a brand of Christian anarchism and Gide ultimately on atheism, perhaps accounting for some of their fundamental differences. For Tolstoy, man must turn toward God and follow the callings of his conscience; for Gide man must set out on the open seas, abandoning the shore in search of something true within himself. Gide may not have been a good artist in the view of Tolstoy. And for similar reason, many would reject this moral author on grounds of immorality. And no one could really fault them for doing so. His work has strong stylistic merits and his influence is notable (as mentioned throughout), but reading this work (and the same goes for other works of his, I'm assured) one must be prepared to be disturbed, very deeply disturbed. Is this good art? I don't really know. There's a lot that is distasteful in this work (maybe a little too much). But, then, the same could be said for many a Greek tragedy and work of Shakespeare (which Tolstoy did not consider good art). And yet stylistically and thematically there is also a lot to admire. Just don't expect much in terms of reassurance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6935559345978905491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer541654104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10937812145728532904">An outright coming of age story with the young Bernard Profitendieu as a central figure. There is a broad spectrum of characters in this novel, some of which are clearly good but very uncertain persons (Edward, Laura...), others really bad (Vincent, Georges), and most are just pretending. Edward is struggling to write a novel, and tries to cope with life. The middle part is the best one, the last part rather messy. The theme of the homosexual attraction is clearly present, but always disguised. </span>
  <span id="freeText10937812145728532904" style="display:none">An outright coming of age story with the young Bernard Profitendieu as a central figure. There is a broad spectrum of characters in this novel, some of which are clearly good but very uncertain persons (Edward, Laura...), others really bad (Vincent, Georges), and most are just pretending. Edward is struggling to write a novel, and tries to cope with life. The middle part is the best one, the last part rather messy. The theme of the homosexual attraction is clearly present, but always disguised. Stylistically this is modernist for sure, but not as revolutionary as predecessor Conrad and follower Faulkner. This certainly is the "richest" novel by Gide, but not fully succesfull.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11450858301362073675">متد کتاب:<br/>"I invent the character of a novelist, whom I make my central figure; and the subject of the book, if you must have one, is just that very struggle between what reality offers him and what he himself desires to make of it."<br/>جملات محبوبم:<br/>"One doesn’t discover new lands without consenting to lose sight of the shore for a very long time."<br/><br/>"In real life nothing is solved; everything continues. We remain in our uncertainty; and we shall remain to the very end without knowing what to make of th</span>
  <span id="freeText11450858301362073675" style="display:none">متد کتاب:<br />"I invent the character of a novelist, whom I make my central figure; and the subject of the book, if you must have one, is just that very struggle between what reality offers him and what he himself desires to make of it."<br />جملات محبوبم:<br />"One doesn’t discover new lands without consenting to lose sight of the shore for a very long time."<br /><br />"In real life nothing is solved; everything continues. We remain in our uncertainty; and we shall remain to the very end without knowing what to make of things."<br /><br />"That was no doubt the reason he cared so little for society—he had none of the qualities which are necessary to shine in it."<br /><br />"Nothing is good for everyone, but only relatively to some people; that nothing is true for everyone, but only relatively to the person who believes it is; that there is no method and no theory which can be applied indifferently to all alike; that if, in order to act, we must make a choice, at any rate we are free to choose; and that if we aren’t free to choose, the thing is simpler still; the belief that becomes truth for me (not absolutely, no doubt, but relatively to me) is that which allows me the best use of my strength, the best means of putting my virtues into action...It's a long way that leads from what I thought I was to what perhaps I really am."<br /><br />" I may doubt the reality of everything, but not the reality of my doubt."<br /><br />این جالب بود برام که برنار به هر عقیده‌ای بر‌می‌خورد تو یه صفحه‌ از دفترچه‌ش یادداشتش می‌کرد و صفحه ی مقابل رو می‌ذاشت واسه عقیده‌ی متضاد.</span>

<div id="review_3411840486" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3411840486" />
    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3411840486?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luís" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luís" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s">Luís</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3411840486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11670101229884921367">The Counterfeiters is the chief novel of the work of the French author André Gide. It tells the story of a multitude of characters, each of which symbolizes the different incarnations of the author. Declaims all philosophical theories on the novel and art and human relations and develops them up to convince the reader, just before continuing in another character (taken in a new context generally) an idea that has everything contradictory and which appears to us just as right. In the end, the sto</span>
  <span id="freeText11670101229884921367" style="display:none">The Counterfeiters is the chief novel of the work of the French author André Gide. It tells the story of a multitude of characters, each of which symbolizes the different incarnations of the author. Declaims all philosophical theories on the novel and art and human relations and develops them up to convince the reader, just before continuing in another character (taken in a new context generally) an idea that has everything contradictory and which appears to us just as right. In the end, the story shows the meeting of various personalities of the author. The story in itself is that of two young friends a few weeks before their examination of the baccalaureate which, leaving high school in the 1920s, live an adventure that could describe as astonishing literary. Although it seems madly humiliating to reduce the story to the story of these two heroes, because around them, it is a whole web of heroes who have their own stories. But all these stories have a link between them, a link for the characters, family or friends or knowledge, for example, Bernard and Edward who meet by the chances of their two adventures, they realize that they both have a link with another character, Olivier's. They will continue the experience together, but we will have their points of view to both, on each side, which will give us the impression of two parallel paths rather than a shared history. The world of Edouard and the family of Olivier, that of Laura and pension Azaïs and finally that of Profitendieu mingle in one world, the world of some good Parisian families, a pension of students, the younger generation of these families, the literary spirit and freedom of this youth. <br />In the inter-war that exploits this novel, we applaud a fair and exciting painting of the freedom of spirit, of creation. Of the artistic movement of that time when we caught our breath of war passed and where the emotion was tending to fall back, where we were finally rebuilding, and where morals tend to liberalize. <br />In the novel, the author gives us to see a literary meeting where we see some significant figures of the time, like Alfred Jarry. The latter looks at this moment of a man overbearing, crazy and slightly offbeat reality by this original character that he plays in society. Overall we follow the vision of characteristics who seek to create journals, to write, to poise, and finally to revolutionize the classical form of writing. A character, by the way, named Strouvilhou launches a sketch of the revolutionary ideas that run through the arts at that time, we see him theorizing what could be the surrealists' desires overall. <br />Thus, all of Gide's characters seem pursued by greatness and romantic thrill. All seem poets and artists. <br />There is, therefore, a lively youth, adults with intelligence that lost more than everyday life, novelists. Characters tied and endearing. Two figures stand out from the crowd. However, it is Lady Griffith and the Count of Passavant who seem to be the evil gods and manipulators of this story. They are idle people, who give their esteem only to those who are useful or agreeable to them but without any sound or reasonable foundation. <br />In this novel, finally, there is an intelligence's struggle of those who detach themselves to carry on unknown lands, those struggling to get there, and those who believe arriving but in reality, circling. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11670101229884921367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3447267605" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3447267605" />
    <a title="rosamund" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51041100-rosamund"><img alt="rosamund" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540585649p2/51041100.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3447267605?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="rosamund" class="user" itemprop="url" name="rosamund" href="/user/show/51041100-rosamund">rosamund</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140180930" href="/book/show/70106.The_Counterfeiters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51041100-rosamund?shelf=nobel-prize-winner">nobel-prize-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3447267605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12996076701397664581">A novel about writing a novel called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, this is a picaresque, discursive novel centering on a group of boys who are in their final year of school, and various authors who encourage them. At the heart of the story is Edouard, who is trying to write a book called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, and is in love with his young half-nephew, Olivier. Perhaps the other major character is Bernard, who discovers he was born following his mother having an adulterous affair, and runs away from home. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText12996076701397664581" style="display:none">A novel about writing a novel called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, this is a picaresque, discursive novel centering on a group of boys who are in their final year of school, and various authors who encourage them. At the heart of the story is Edouard, who is trying to write a book called <i>The Counterfeiters</i>, and is in love with his young half-nephew, Olivier. Perhaps the other major character is Bernard, who discovers he was born following his mother having an adulterous affair, and runs away from home. There are many other important characters, including school teachers, judges, psychiatrists and even the occasional woman (!). There is also the narrator, who observes everything and knows everything, and is not the same person as Edouard. This is a very complex story, full of metatextual references, as well as plot twists and new characters. It hangs together because Gide is such an exceptional prose stylist, and he finds beauty and clarity in the most convoluted of plots. His themes include despair and degradation, but this is ultimately an optimistic book, although it has a somewhat mawkish final twist. The positive treatment of gay characters is refreshing for a novel from 1924. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12996076701397664581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2639964576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6228366198124449520">Gide has a complex plot and proposes a different style for writing a novel compared to most previous novels. Several stories are intertwined, interact at some moments and then some of them are forgot or fade away. Each new moment changes the point of view presented in the previous sequences. As Edouard says when discussing the topic of his novel, it's the fight between the facts proposed by the reality (the novel, in my opinion) and the ideal reality. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_807148683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/807148683" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5928275-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/807148683?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/5928275-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5928275-howard?shelf=naughty-classic">naughty-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5928275-howard?shelf=europe">europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer807148683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14650242821480961024">This is the surprising interwoven and sexually obscure tale of young men in Paris around the turn of the twentieth century. It was written in 1925 and is a rated classic for the `book within a book' style.<br/><br/>Uncle Edouard (38) (Gide styled and as a first person journal) is an author in competition with the notorious, fellow author Robert de Passvant. His half-sister Pauline Molinier, married to Oscar, has sons Olivier, Vincent and George (14). The sons are at college with Olivier befriending Bernar</span>
  <span id="freeText14650242821480961024" style="display:none">This is the surprising interwoven and sexually obscure tale of young men in Paris around the turn of the twentieth century. It was written in 1925 and is a rated classic for the `book within a book' style.<br /><br />Uncle Edouard (38) (Gide styled and as a first person journal) is an author in competition with the notorious, fellow author Robert de Passvant. His half-sister Pauline Molinier, married to Oscar, has sons Olivier, Vincent and George (14). The sons are at college with Olivier befriending Bernard Profitendieu, who has learnt that he's a bastard and left home. Vincent has got Laura Videl, married to professor Felix, pregnant but equally Lilian Griffith conspires with Robert to win Vincent. Robert and Edouard are each seemingly using their friendships, planning literary journals needing editors and their foreign travels to get close to the young men. The young men become in-love and jealous. There are many non-graphic but literary scenes suggesting or otherwise of guys sharing beds and time together in ambiguous and not so ambiguous gay/bisexual ways. There are many back stories based around the Laura's sibling Armand, Rachael and Sarah; the ex-college teachers of Edouard, the Azais. Middleclass parents have to deal (badly) with the issues including counterfeiting and a young man run brothel club.<br /><br />This is a mix of Middlemarch, Gissing's Nether World and Murger's Bohemians of the Latin Quarter but heavy on the sexually ambivalent. It's quite existential actually.<br /><br />Some good quotes and there many:<br /><br />"Vincent walks home, meditating as he goes; he realises that from the satisfaction of desire there may arise, accompanying joy and as it were sheltering behind it, something not unlike despair"<br /><br />"I am constantly getting out of myself, and as I watch myself act I cannot understand how a person who acts is the same as the person who is watching him act, and who wonders in astonishment and doubt how he can be actor and watcher at the same moment"<br /><br />"The deeper the soul plunges into religious devotion, the more it loses all sense of reality, all need, all desire, all love for reality"<br /><br />"If one could recover the uncompromising spirit of one's youth, one's greatest indignation would be for what one has become"<br /><br />"Well, you must allow it's a melancholy business when love, instead of making the happiness of life, becomes its calamity.... That's no doubt the way God loves us"<br /><br />"And if I can't endure the thought of a Christ sacrificing himself for the thankless salvation of all frightful people I knock up against daily, I imagine with some satisfaction, and indeed a kind of serenity, the rotting of that vile mob in order to produce a Christ"<br /><br />This is a really modern feeling classic; and is the second Gide novel I've read having enjoyed the Immoralist. Both well worth reading but Counterfeiters is the lengthier better. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14650242821480961024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
